  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_87e9da74-c352-43c8-ba8c-edf65590d9c3'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer15768702357901185541">In <i>Pilot Pirx</i>, Lem has created an irresistibly likable character: an astronaut who gives the impression of still navigating by the seat of his pants-a bumbler but an inspired one. By investing Pirx with a range of human foibles, Lem offers a wonderful vision of the audacity, childlike curiosity, and intuition that can give humans the courage to confront outer space. Transl</span>
  <span id="freeText15768702357901185541" style="display:none">In <i>Pilot Pirx</i>, Lem has created an irresistibly likable character: an astronaut who gives the impression of still navigating by the seat of his pants-a bumbler but an inspired one. By investing Pirx with a range of human foibles, Lem offers a wonderful vision of the audacity, childlike curiosity, and intuition that can give humans the courage to confront outer space. Translated by Louis Iribarne.<br /><br />A Helen and Kurt Wolff Book.</span>

<div id="review_204537595" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/204537595" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2201886-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1239392797p2/2201886.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/204537595?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/2201886-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer204537595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1767377678934565229">I remember reading a bunch of Stanislaw Lem quite a while ago. "Tales of Pirx The Pilot" was certainly one of them and it was with some fondness that I picked up another copy while looking for another specific book. I am happy to report that I was not disappointed. This book is a solid "4.0".<br/><br/>These stories of Pirx are an odd amalgamation of interesting science and young/juvenile action-adventure, but done in a very readable and convincing way. Without revealing too much, I found that Lem's testin</span>
  <span id="freeText1767377678934565229" style="display:none">I remember reading a bunch of Stanislaw Lem quite a while ago. "Tales of Pirx The Pilot" was certainly one of them and it was with some fondness that I picked up another copy while looking for another specific book. I am happy to report that I was not disappointed. This book is a solid "4.0".<br /><br />These stories of Pirx are an odd amalgamation of interesting science and young/juvenile action-adventure, but done in a very readable and convincing way. Without revealing too much, I found that Lem's testing of the cadets to be a rather obvious requirement for any real deep-space or extended isolation mission even though he does it in a highly imaginative and unorthodox way. Great stuff. And the character of Pirx is appealing, too. He's an everyman training to become a leader, with all of the doubts and foibles that ordinary men and women have. Despite the space background these stories are ultimately about the character and his development in each of the tales.<br /><br />Although I probably won't buy any further copies, this slim volume has rekindled my appreciation for the works of Mr. Lem. I'll be re-visiting more of his works in the future. If you've never read any of his works then youo have missed some very good writing. While the stories are short and superficially simple, they are clever and show a deep understanding of human psychology. I strongly recommend them.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1767377678934565229" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3494695260" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3494695260" />
    <a title="Oblomov" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/113963239-oblomov"><img alt="Oblomov" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591234334p2/113963239.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3494695260?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Oblomov" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Oblomov" href="/user/show/113963239-oblomov">Oblomov</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/113963239-oblomov?shelf=5-beatniks-to-punks">5-beatniks-to-punks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3494695260" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1337311704238170114">Having read Lem's superb <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18194.The_Cyberiad' title='The Cyberiad by Stanis≈Çaw Lem' rel='nofollow noopener'> The Cyberiad</a>, the heartbreaking <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/95558.Solaris' title='Solaris by Stanis≈Çaw Lem' rel='nofollow noopener'> Solaris</a> and the fun yet often dark Ijon Tichy tales, now was the time to meet another of his stalwarts, with the rough but plucky, luckless yet sharp Pilot Pirx, and if that contradictory and trite description of the character hasn't tipped you off, I wasn't a massive fan.<br/><br/>First there's the writing:<br/>Lem goes for the straight and hard science fiction here. There's no aliens or fully sentient androids, and the extremely fucking long descript</span>
  <span id="freeText1337311704238170114" style="display:none">Having read Lem's superb <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18194.The_Cyberiad" title="The Cyberiad by Stanis≈Çaw Lem" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Cyberiad</a>, the heartbreaking <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/95558.Solaris" title="Solaris by Stanis≈Çaw Lem" rel="nofollow noopener"> Solaris</a> and the fun yet often dark Ijon Tichy tales, now was the time to meet another of his stalwarts, with the rough but plucky, luckless yet sharp Pilot Pirx, and if that contradictory and trite description of the character hasn't tipped you off, I wasn't a massive fan.<br /><br />First there's the writing:<br />Lem goes for the straight and hard science fiction here. There's no aliens or fully sentient androids, and the extremely fucking long descriptions of spacecraft systems and proceedures seemed quite sound (as far as I'm aware anyway, he could be describing flux capacitors or 'the spice' in scientific jargon and my dumb brain wouldn't know). <i>Solaris</i> had those long descriptions too, but the book fully earned and needed it besides the compelling human drama and taste of cosmic horror, while here it's just walls and walls of text about levers and engine power, the pace slowed in a claggy bog of 'realism'.<br />There are still moments of extremely visercal and creepy imagery buried amongst the Rocket Flier's Manual, mainly in 'On Patrol' and 'Terminus', arguably the best stories in this collection, but the others (forgive me, Master Lem) were not only boring but had little pay off due to the book's second problem:<br /><br />The plots:<br />Lem often throws philosophical conundrums into his works, like concepts of personhood, sentience, memory, love or 'what has science done!' roars into the void, but all that is missing from this book. Bar one moment of reflection in the last story, there's nothing much here that tests your brain besides the jargon; it's just Pirx as a mostly bored space pilot who occassionally face plants into mysteries. Despite the unexplained dead or the odd scrape, I didn't find the tales especially compelling, and the answer to the puzzles were usually a let down, with two of these five tales having the cheek to basically end with the same bloody 'twist'.<br />The life and mishaps of a space pilot written in all its dull and mundane 'reality' could still have been interesting in a distorted, slice of life sort of way, if it weren't for the third problem with this book:<br /><br />Pirx the <s>Pissant</s> Pilot. I just could not like this prat. He's quite hapless in most cases, yet still manages to solve some mysteries because he just so happens to stumble upon that one clue everyone else missed. Don't imagine that makes him a sci-fi Sherlock by the way, it's more remembering the torch so you don't fall down the gully like the last poor three sods. Pirx thinks outside of the box, mainly for his own self-preservation's sake, but his main 'skill' seems to be curiosity and hindsight, and reading him think 'what did X who horribly died <i>not</i> do?' gets quite old quite fast. Pirx feels embyronic in this book, there's hints of a later more rugged and seasoned greatness, but none of these stories show that development well for me.<br />As for his actual character, the man himself, he's a dick. I couldn't tell you of his tastes, his family, his past or personality past a charmless, grumbling git, so any sparks of genius he shows seem out of place or the result of possible possession by a more competent character. I finished the book feeling a little for his brief glimpses of humanity but not much desire to read more.<br /><br />Two stars are for 'Terminus' and 'On Patrol', both of which I'd still reccomend if only in isolation, and the third is a concession as, naff main character aside, I know I'm reading out of my comfort zone here. It feels like I'm complaining a foreign film is shite simply because it has no subtitles, as the book's granite science and space bureacracy is aimed at an audience I'm not in. If you're a fan of sci-fi so dense it would suck in Jupiter, then Pirx will provide, but any fans of <i>Dune</i> or even Lem's Ijon Tichy stories may find themselves on a dull ride.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1337311704238170114" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2967262786" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2967262786" />
    <a title="Victor Sonkin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1647517-victor-sonkin"><img alt="Victor Sonkin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442503953p2/1647517.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2967262786?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Victor Sonkin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Victor Sonkin" href="/user/show/1647517-victor-sonkin">Victor Sonkin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9785170591923" href="/book/show/18107365">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1647517-victor-sonkin?shelf=civilization">civilization</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1647517-victor-sonkin?shelf=european">european</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1647517-victor-sonkin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1647517-victor-sonkin?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1647517-victor-sonkin?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1647517-victor-sonkin?shelf=cognitive">cognitive</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1647517-victor-sonkin?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1647517-victor-sonkin?shelf=aviation">aviation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1647517-victor-sonkin?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2967262786" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9214656769688343465">This collection is thought-provoking and deep. At the same time it should be clear that this is not the kind of science fiction that predicts the future; it sets philosophical or ethical problems of the present against the rather abstract background of space travel (which is very matter-of-fact and mundane). The sociological reality of the world remains exactly as it was in the later half of the 20th century: USSR and the Western bloc, everyone smokes, virtually no women anywhere (except as pass</span>
  <span id="freeText9214656769688343465" style="display:none">This collection is thought-provoking and deep. At the same time it should be clear that this is not the kind of science fiction that predicts the future; it sets philosophical or ethical problems of the present against the rather abstract background of space travel (which is very matter-of-fact and mundane). The sociological reality of the world remains exactly as it was in the later half of the 20th century: USSR and the Western bloc, everyone smokes, virtually no women anywhere (except as passengers or secretaries), people haul large sacks of books with them, etc.<br />I tried to read the stories in Polish, but they are so full of technical jargon (which is nothing more than a filler) that it was very exhausting; I managed to read some fragments and ascertain that the Russian translation was very exact.<br /><br />The stories are:<br />1. The Test (–ò—Å–ø—ã—Ç–∞–Ω–∏–µ). Pirx, a student, is given the task of piloting a rocket to the Moon; he barely escapes a disaster, and then finds out that the test was staged, –ú–æ—Å–∫–≤–∞‚Äî–ö–∞—Å—Å–∏–æ–ø–µ—è style. The other student who was 'flying', everyone's favorite, failed the test and 'crashed' into the Moon.<br />2. Conditional reflex (–£—Å–ª–æ–≤–Ω—ã–π —Ä–µ—Ñ–ª–µ–∫—Å). Pirx passes a sensory deprivation test with flying colors and is sent to the Moon with a dangerous mission (with another guy called Langner), since the previous couple of researchers had died a mysterious death. When Pirx goes to change photoplates, the exact same thing happens to them, and it takes all of Pirx's calmness and level-headedness to avoid the same result.<br />3. Patrol (–ü–∞—Ç—Ä—É–ª—å). When patrolling an abandoned and empty sector of the Solar System, where two patrolling officers had disappeared lately, Pirx finds a mysterious object and is overwhelmed by a horrible physiological phenomenon; the object turns out to be illusory, the phenomenon later accepted as a regular one in such situations.<br />4. Albatross (–ê–ª—å–±–∞—Ç—Ä–æ—Å). Pirx accidentally travels on a luxury cruise, which receives a SOS from another spaceship. Pirx goes to the ship's central cab and follows the disaster; the crew cannot offer any meaningful help, and the cruiser soon resumes its regular operations.<br />5. Terminus (–¢–µ—Ä–º–∏–Ω—É—Å). While commanding an old decrepit spaceship, Pirx finds out it was the one that had catastrophically disintegrated during one of its previous missions. The only survivor was Terminus, a primitive robot, who's still on the ship. While sealing the tubes from radiation, Terminus uses a Morse code which is the exact replicas of the code apparently transmitted to each other by the members of the dying crew of the fateful expedition. Terminus is completely unaware of that. Pirx Morses something in reply, and Terminus's hands produce a violent response, as if the cosmonauts were still alive. At a loss, somewhat disgusted, Pirx orders to write off the robot for demolition.<br />6. The Hunt (–û—Ö–æ—Ç–∞). While on the Moon, Pirx is summoned to hunt for a robot called Setaurus who had escaped from the storage after a meteor hit and apparently became extremely violent. The robot kills a small crew of people hunting it, and then Pirx and his colleague MacCork find it and try to lure it out. In the end, in the confrontation, Pirx is almost killed by friendly fire, but Setaurus saves him; in response, Pirx accidentally kills the robot.<br />7. Pirx's Story (–†–∞—Å—Å–∫–∞–∑ –ü–∏—Ä–∫—Å–∞). A first-person story of an expedition when Pirx found an apparently extremely old intergalactic spaceship of a different civilization, recorded everything and then found out that there was no tape in the recorder.<br />8. An Accident (–ù–µ—Å—á–∞—Å—Ç–Ω—ã–π —Å–ª—É—á–∞–π). Preparing to depart from a terramorph planet, the crew loses its robot, Anel. The three of them go to look for it and find out that it started climbing the mountain; after an arduous climb, they discover that the robot had plunged down and crashed.<br />9. The Inquest (–î–æ–∑–Ω–∞–Ω–∏–µ). Pirx is given the task of flying a crew which partially consists of robots, but he doesn't know who's who. During a complex operation, the robot attempts a dangerous manoeuvre which would kill all people and leave 'him' alive, but mistakes Pirx's perplexity for a cunning plan and makes the fatal mistake.<br />10. Ananke (–ê–Ω–∞–Ω–∫–µ). A rocket crashes while landing on Mars; Pirx finds out that it was due to the paranoid nature of the engineer who had programmed it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9214656769688343465" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_10892894" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10892894" />
    <a title="Krista the Krazy Kataloguer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/316102-krista-the-krazy-kataloguer"><img alt="Krista the Krazy Kataloguer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1191519600p2/316102.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10892894?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krista the Krazy Kataloguer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krista the Krazy Kataloguer" href="/user/show/316102-krista-the-krazy-kataloguer">Krista the Krazy Kataloguer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/316102-krista-the-krazy-kataloguer?shelf=read-sci-fi-fantasy">read-sci-fi-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/316102-krista-the-krazy-kataloguer?shelf=read-adult-fiction">read-adult-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10892894" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14709085871529164816">Stanislaw Lem's science fiction tends to be dense and philosophical/political. This book was different, in that the adventures of Pirx, an average sap who stumbles into these situations, are based on hard science and yet retain that philosophic air. I enjoyed this book very much-- must read it again!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1152797177" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1152797177" />
    <a title="Wanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9114047-wanda"><img alt="Wanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468169847p2/9114047.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1152797177?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wanda" href="/user/show/9114047-wanda">Wanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1152797177" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8129121559908409890">I couldn't stop reading this book! Lem's sci-fi is funny and quirky, yet still wicked smart. A highly plausible imagining of future space travel, I was completely enthralled by the descriptions of alien landscapes and rocket ships alike. I must admit that I was far from compelled by Pirx upon his introduction, but grew to enjoy his straightforward nature and the development of his competence. My only complaint is that a couple of the stories tend towards the formulaic: Pirx encounters some stran</span>
  <span id="freeText8129121559908409890" style="display:none">I couldn't stop reading this book! Lem's sci-fi is funny and quirky, yet still wicked smart. A highly plausible imagining of future space travel, I was completely enthralled by the descriptions of alien landscapes and rocket ships alike. I must admit that I was far from compelled by Pirx upon his introduction, but grew to enjoy his straightforward nature and the development of his competence. My only complaint is that a couple of the stories tend towards the formulaic: Pirx encounters some strange, almost supernatural phenomenon, whereby uses his highly developed imagination and reasoning skills to approach the problem in a novel way to come to some happy conclusion. That being said, I believe Lem captures human emotion on these new frontiers effortlessly, from "the dip" to the boundless horizon of space.<br /><br />I did have one complaint specific to the way in which one story, the final one, was completed. I felt somewhat put out that Pirx had Terminus slated for dismantling, without really investigating its functioning... I mean, shouldn't the robot looking for the cat have been a sign of higher functioning, especially since he couldn't express with the pre-programmed cues what his purpose for doing so was? Further, when Pirx communicated via Morse code, he seemed to illicit and unexpected response; why all this talk about multiple personas during human sleep if he could so casually dismiss the thought of Terminus' sentience! I felt let down, since I believe that Lem was more than capable of a coherent exposition on the theory of artificial intelligence as it applied to the story :( Still, I was haunted by the echoes of disaster, preserved only in Morse, and the decrepitude of the ship was well conveyed, to eerie effect! Pretty brilliant writing all around :D<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8129121559908409890" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_134609722" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/134609722" />
    <a title="John" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/744209-john"><img alt="John" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496029007p2/744209.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/134609722?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/744209-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151879786" href="/book/show/525366.Tales_of_Pirx_the_Pilot">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/744209-john?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/744209-john?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer134609722" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6337675556038890596">Holy Spaceballs! Lem is the master. <br/><br/>First off these loosely connected stories, that span over the progression of Pirx's noteworthy career as a space pilot, are meditations on the smallness of the individual's contribution to the overall adventure of human history. Pirx is just a normal, regular guy. Except that within the emptiness of his every person's brain exists a banality of common sense, but unlike the simpleton that usually embodies such a mindset, Pirx is actually a thoughtful problem so</span>
  <span id="freeText6337675556038890596" style="display:none">Holy Spaceballs! Lem is the master. <br /><br />First off these loosely connected stories, that span over the progression of Pirx's noteworthy career as a space pilot, are meditations on the smallness of the individual's contribution to the overall adventure of human history. Pirx is just a normal, regular guy. Except that within the emptiness of his every person's brain exists a banality of common sense, but unlike the simpleton that usually embodies such a mindset, Pirx is actually a thoughtful problem solver. But the problems he tackles are ones of luck and survival, or figuring out what to do with a disturbing outmoded robot.<br /><br />Pirx's innate charm is his stumbling ability to improvise and worry his way through mysteries that have baffled better detectives. For instance, in "The Conditioned Reflex," when he is confronted with the same disappearing colleague, that doomed the previous mission - Pirx does the opposite of what he imagines the dead crew did. It is this sort of thinking beyond his training that makes Pirx a hero. Though he is a hero without the fame and mythology that marks so much of the genre's most tired tropes. Pirx pokes holes in the fables and myths of exceptionalism. <br /><br />Meanwhile, Lem proves himself exceptional once again. He excels, in this novel, when describing the basic training of Pirx the Pilot. The sensory deprivation tank section is a few pages of sheer altered consciousness/body horror. It was a visceral and awesome description of travelling - first into and then beyond the body. Masterful.<br /><br />Loved it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6337675556038890596" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1215058608" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1215058608" />
    <a title="Timothy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9954273-timothy"><img alt="Timothy" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1215058608?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Timothy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Timothy" href="/user/show/9954273-timothy">Timothy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780380556656" href="/book/show/251646.Tales_of_Pirx_the_Pilot">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1215058608" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10387895602404487522">Pirx is not the best I have read by Lem, but, for me, these simple tales are very satisfying. The progression is chronological but with large gaps as Lem captures interesting episodes of Prix career from young cadet to full-fledged navigator. Over time, Pirx loses his air of the preening day-dreamer spinning fantasies of glory. While Prix grows into his profession and into adulthood, Lem never let's us forget that space flight is a serious business and that the cost of getting better at it is of</span>
  <span id="freeText10387895602404487522" style="display:none">Pirx is not the best I have read by Lem, but, for me, these simple tales are very satisfying. The progression is chronological but with large gaps as Lem captures interesting episodes of Prix career from young cadet to full-fledged navigator. Over time, Pirx loses his air of the preening day-dreamer spinning fantasies of glory. While Prix grows into his profession and into adulthood, Lem never let's us forget that space flight is a serious business and that the cost of getting better at it is often gained in the postmortem of events that went tragically awry. Pirx is not the brightest tool in the shed, but he is steady and keeps his head in a scrape. While never achieving the almost elemental status of Conrad's captains who reveal their metal when the storm is raging, Prix muddles his way through and his instincts are good.<br /><br />Lem keeps the tales simple but he knows how to find small seams of gold. Lem just clicks for me and did the very first time I read him. I could never recommend Lem with great confidence to another reader without some understanding of their sensibilities. As for me, I like the quirkiness, the wryness, the balancing on a razor's edge between the real and what lies just on the other side of it. Most of all, Lem never loses sight of the human being(s) at the heart of all good stories.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10387895602404487522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13358612221099472512"><em>Tales of Pirx the Pilot</em> has much of the humor of Lem‚Äôs other works without the complexity. Pirx is an Everyman, he‚Äôs not a hero like many of his literary colleagues though he gets the job done, nor a comedic bumbler like <em>Bill the Galactic Hero</em>, though he has his share of bumbles. He‚Äôs a working-class pilot, going to what appears to be pilot trade-school, learning his craft, and taking his suitcase into his first commercial ship more like a factory worker with his lunch pail than a modern jet pil</span>
  <span id="freeText13358612221099472512" style="display:none"><em>Tales of Pirx the Pilot</em> has much of the humor of Lem‚Äôs other works without the complexity. Pirx is an Everyman, he‚Äôs not a hero like many of his literary colleagues though he gets the job done, nor a comedic bumbler like <em>Bill the Galactic Hero</em>, though he has his share of bumbles. He‚Äôs a working-class pilot, going to what appears to be pilot trade-school, learning his craft, and taking his suitcase into his first commercial ship more like a factory worker with his lunch pail than a modern jet pilot.<br /><br />It‚Äôs practically steampunk now, although it wouldn‚Äôt have been thought so at the time.<br /><br />His matter-of-fact description of hiking on the moon makes me sad that only now are we opening up space travel to commercial activity.<br /><br />His robot semi-ghost story doesn‚Äôt even end. It just leaves us wondering the same thing Pirx is.<br /><br />This would, I think, be a great introduction to Stanislaw Lem, and it‚Äôs definitely a fun read.<br /><br />I also enjoy the cover of the version I read. At first glance, it looks like a pilot in a space suit, but after reading the first story, all the strange details start coming out: the flies, the book, and so on.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7525190144998328077">I got a collection of three different Lem novels at the Oxfam. This was the first, the back had it listed as mysteries. It really wasn't. It was very technical 50s science fiction written in the late 70s. It was another case of In the Future There Are No Women! Which was a bit disappointing. Even in translation though the stories had a very clear and visual style. My problem was that I found Pirx terribly dull, stereotypical young pilot, and there were no real other characters in the book. I'm g</span>
  <span id="freeText7525190144998328077" style="display:none">I got a collection of three different Lem novels at the Oxfam. This was the first, the back had it listed as mysteries. It really wasn't. It was very technical 50s science fiction written in the late 70s. It was another case of In the Future There Are No Women! Which was a bit disappointing. Even in translation though the stories had a very clear and visual style. My problem was that I found Pirx terribly dull, stereotypical young pilot, and there were no real other characters in the book. I'm glad I stuck with it though as the last story, Terminus was generally very creepy and disturbing and I really liked it, and the imagery, though less than satisfied with the ending. I will definitely try reading the other two novels though as they sound very different, though maybe not right away.</span>

  <span id="freeText12243704711189182573" style="display:none">While I didn't enjoy these stories as much as the Ijon Tichy series (still two books to read in that series, one of which is sadly still untranslated) they're still worth reading.     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1653752167" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1653752167" />
    <a title="William Leight" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26434330-william-leight"><img alt="William Leight" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1653752167?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William Leight" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William Leight" href="/user/show/26434330-william-leight">William Leight</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1653752167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5527037389863673337">‚ÄúTales of Pirx the Pilot‚Äù takes a restrained approach to science fiction, one which could almost be described as ‚Äúrealism‚Äù except for the nuclear-powered rocket ships, moon bases, and robots. The book consists of five episodes from the life of Pirx, a rather ordinary guy who becomes a rather ordinary rocket pilot, with the first two stories happening while he is in training and the next three from his career. Pirx is not the best rocket pilot ever: he does not defend earth against alien invasion</span>
  <span id="freeText5527037389863673337" style="display:none">‚ÄúTales of Pirx the Pilot‚Äù takes a restrained approach to science fiction, one which could almost be described as ‚Äúrealism‚Äù except for the nuclear-powered rocket ships, moon bases, and robots. The book consists of five episodes from the life of Pirx, a rather ordinary guy who becomes a rather ordinary rocket pilot, with the first two stories happening while he is in training and the next three from his career. Pirx is not the best rocket pilot ever: he does not defend earth against alien invasion or discover new worlds or fight in wars. Instead, he flies routine patrols, takes long, boring shifts at a moon base, and gets assigned to fly an ancient rustbucket. In the fourth story, "The Albatross", Pirx doesn‚Äôt even do anything: he merely happens to be a passenger on board a rocket ship which is at first directed to come to the aid of another one whose nuclear core is melting down and then ends up not contributing to any rescue when other ships arrive first. The story consists almost entirely of Pirx listening to radio transmissions as the rescue is organized and attempted. "The Conditioned Reflex" is perhaps most representative of what Lem is trying to do. It opens with a long description of Pirx‚Äôs experience in a sensory-deprivation tank (a standard part of rocket-pilot training), at which he does very well (though he does not discover any unusual abilities or anything like that). Shortly afterwards, he is summoned to meet with the head of the Academy, who quickly squashes his excitement by announcing that he wants Pirx to take a months-long shift at a two-man astronomical observatory on the moon. The previous occupants died under mysterious circumstances, true, but it was almost certainly just bad luck: however, the people who run the moon-base have asked for a pilot to help take the next shift anyway, and Pirx has been chosen. His trip to the moon-base is a further expression of mundanity: nobody is impressed by Pirx‚Äôs status as an almost-pilot, the rocket he takes to the moon is full of tourists, and no detail of the bureaucracy of arriving on the moon and transiting to a shuttle that will take him to his destination (including the indignity of being unable to get a spacesuit that fits him) is neglected. On the way, there‚Äôs a stop-over at a Russian moon base for a discussion of how international politics led to the collapse of the road leading to the smaller base where he will be staying (the only time in the book that nationalities are mentioned at all, the name Pirx being almost certainly deliberately chosen to not sound like any particular nationality). Most of his time at the base consists of uneventful routine. And to cap it off, the story begins and ends with Pirx (rather to his disgust) being described as a ‚Äúrather ordinary fellow‚Äù. It is, surprisingly, rather reminiscent of the slice-of-life storytelling style of somebody like Alice Munro.<br /><br />The difference is that there is a mystery to be solved here: as the reader has probably guessed, Pirx will end up discovering (half by accident: he‚Äôs not supposed to be Sherlock Holmes) the reason for the deaths of his predecessors. But the solution is not aliens or international politics but something far simpler: a hardware bug. This is the other aspect of Lem‚Äôs science-fiction realism: his emphasis on the reality of machines. Generally speaking, the technological miracles in science-fiction stories are either working or broken, but they are rarely buggy, even though dealing with technology that is mostly but not entirely or not reliably functional is a key part of everyday life. (As my mother likes to say, the least realistic part of ‚ÄúNeuromancer‚Äù is that no computer in the book ever crashes.) A key part of writing science fiction stories is of course inventing machines, but Lem goes further than most by also inventing ways in which they might fail (importantly, without necessarily seeming to fail) and that failure plays a key role in all the stories. This means that the central conflict and driver of the plot (to the extent that there is a plot) in all the tales is the struggle between man and machine. But there are no robot armies rising up against their human masters here: instead, the struggle is fundamentally the same one that you have when you spend hours trying to figure out why some program on your computer or phone is not doing what it‚Äôs supposed to. The problem is not that the machines in the story are too successful, but rather that, despite being highly sophisticated and precise devices, they remain flawed. The flaws come, of course, from the fact that they were constructed by flawed builders: as a result, the struggle is in fact really just another example of humanity in (in this case inadvertent) conflict with itself. Like all the best science fiction writers, Lem knows that his futuristic devices are most interesting for what they tell us about ourselves.<br /><br />Of course, this requires a relatable and realistic proxy for the reader. The character of Pirx is essential to the success of these stories, especially given the long stretches we spend alone with Pirx and the lack of other significant characters (aside from the machines, that is). Pirx is, as he is described in "The Conditioned Reflex", a rather ordinary guy, but Lem knows not to confuse ‚Äúordinary‚Äù with ‚Äúbland‚Äù. Pirx need not have any special talents to shine in these stories, just some quick thinking and the ability to keep his head in emergencies. "The Conditioned Reflex" is again perhaps the best example here, as Lem provides a perfect sketch of a somewhat dreamy, rather callow, slightly self-important, but mostly likable youth, who manages not to panic and correctly figure out that there‚Äôs a problem with the machine, even if he doesn‚Äôt know what that problem is. Just as important, though, is the way that Pirx changes as he ages. By the last story, he‚Äôs a trained professional, still perhaps a bit dreamy and maybe somewhat more impressionable than average but lacking the inexperience and self-importance of youth. The result is that even in the last two, mostly-plotless, stories, Lem can keep our attention, because Pirx keeps our attention. Though Lem is brilliant at conceiving his future of nuclear-powered rockets, it is the character of Pirx who pushes these stories to the level of classic hard sci-fi.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5527037389863673337" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2456759466" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2456759466" />
    <a title="Mike Moore" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2644896-mike-moore"><img alt="Mike Moore" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2456759466?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike Moore" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike Moore" href="/user/show/2644896-mike-moore">Mike Moore</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2456759466" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9537686551195278122">Stanislaw Lem is a brilliant and innovative author who wrote some of the most thought-provoking science fiction of his time. He also wrote several fairly superficial short stories, some of which featured a bumbling everyman pilot named Pirx. This is a collection of those stories.<br/><br/>The consistent theme here is minor technological problems causing dramatic problems. An unattached panel, multiple computer glitches, bad hydraulics... Pirx is presented as a superior astronaut precisely because he gener</span>
  <span id="freeText9537686551195278122" style="display:none">Stanislaw Lem is a brilliant and innovative author who wrote some of the most thought-provoking science fiction of his time. He also wrote several fairly superficial short stories, some of which featured a bumbling everyman pilot named Pirx. This is a collection of those stories.<br /><br />The consistent theme here is minor technological problems causing dramatic problems. An unattached panel, multiple computer glitches, bad hydraulics... Pirx is presented as a superior astronaut precisely because he generally thinks like a plumber. The stories themselves are trivial and poorly structured, some of them nerve-wrackingly so ("The Conditioned Reflex" is barely a story at all, containing 60 pages of random thoughts about what hanging out on the moon might be like and a 5 page whodunit). The exception is the final story of the collection, Terminus, which deals with the <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=z0NgUhEs1R4" rel="nofollow noopener">effects of trauma on a robot</a> in a story that is fully realized and well structured. Even here the treatment of the device is shallow, with Pirx characteristically concluding "Huh, that's not supposed to happen." However, with that story Lem touches convincingly on deeper themes of man's psychological relationship with his creations. Unfortunately, the rest of the book is much less compelling.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9537686551195278122" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3593199766" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3593199766" />
    <a title="Kelly McCubbin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/287449-kelly-mccubbin"><img alt="Kelly McCubbin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1195589246p2/287449.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3593199766?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly McCubbin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly McCubbin" href="/user/show/287449-kelly-mccubbin">Kelly McCubbin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3593199766" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2417180686980342758">I like Lem's less humorous work, for whatever reason; more "Fiasco" and less "Cyberiad". This falls somewhere in the middle drifting into cheap gags from time to time that just feel slight next to the book's more powerful moments. A lot of this is very strong, though, and the final story, "Terminus" is one of the finest science fiction stories ever written.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_744456584" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/744456584" />
    <a title="Nente" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22056219-nente"><img alt="Nente" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485522372p2/22056219.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/744456584?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nente" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nente" href="/user/show/22056219-nente">Nente</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9785170591923" href="/book/show/18107365">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone who likes their sci-fi thoughtful</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22056219-nente?shelf=the">the</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22056219-nente?shelf=collections">collections</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22056219-nente?shelf=highly-rereadable">highly-rereadable</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22056219-nente?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22056219-nente?shelf=short-story">short-story</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22056219-nente?shelf=bildungs">bildungs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22056219-nente?shelf=geography-challenge">geography-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22056219-nente?shelf=read-translation">read-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22056219-nente?shelf=favourites-one-per-author">favourites-one-per-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22056219-nente?shelf=more-than-once">more-than-once</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer744456584" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4320750920518755808">I am inordinately fond of Stanislaw Lem, as his books were my introduction into the world of quality sci-fi. What he had for (absurdly weak) competition were stilted Soviet writers like Belyaev and Efremov, or adventure-heavy Jules Verne and his more modern followers. Lem was the first to show me that the main, and frequently the only, thing worth looking at in science fiction is not what alien worlds or new technologies await us - it's how we react to them, how we change in response, and what d</span>
  <span id="freeText4320750920518755808" style="display:none">I am inordinately fond of Stanislaw Lem, as his books were my introduction into the world of quality sci-fi. What he had for (absurdly weak) competition were stilted Soviet writers like Belyaev and Efremov, or adventure-heavy Jules Verne and his more modern followers. Lem was the first to show me that the main, and frequently the only, thing worth looking at in science fiction is not what alien worlds or new technologies await us - it's how we react to them, how we change in response, and what doesn't change ever, while we're still human.<br /><br />This collection of short tales exemplifies the best in Lem for me, so it's a big-time favourite with a score of rereads (I mean here all Pirx short stories, but not <i>Fiasko</i> - weird, slow, and not Pirx in any meaningful sense).<br /><br />So, to get it out of the way, the technicalities of the future world are not really believable; it's as if everything stood still and only the space technologies in their narrowest sense advanced. <br />The really powerful computers are still using paper as one of their main outputs, and the pilots still have to draw the expected route on paper using ruler and compasses! There are no women in future. The astronauts smoke even on duty, in a spaceship, where that would basically be air poisoning and an additional strain on climate-control systems!<br />Morse code is used for radio communication, which I could live with in principle, but it is mostly received and decoded by humans, not machines (like I suppose whenever radio communication first started).<br />Much of that may be excused by invoking the "written 50 years ago" card. (though not smoking and not "no women")<br /><br />But it's when we come to psychology and philosophy that you know you're in presence of a master. The loneliness of space is explored, its tragical and comical effects; the interaction and relations between humans and various technology - from simple automatons to artificial intelligence - get looked at from so many angles. How intelligent is AI? How close to humans? What may we use it for and what are the moral limitations we should impose, both on it and on ourselves? "But so could a robot believe in God," famously says Pirx in this book.<br /><br />I should advise everyone to read the stories more or less chronologically, because the main character develops significantly - the stories cover his entire career as an astronaut, from a wet behind the ears, romantically inclined youth, to a skeptical, cautious and firmly principled old space wolf, regarded by new generation almost as a fossil, but relied upon for a critical decision.<br /><br />Some of the ideas you meet here have been done better in other works. But for a quick and comprehensive introduction to Lem, I definitely recommend this (then <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/95558.Solaris" title="Solaris by Stanis≈Çaw Lem" rel="nofollow noopener">Solaris</a>, then <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1282668.The_Futurological_Congress" title="The Futurological Congress by Stanis≈Çaw Lem" rel="nofollow noopener">The Futurological Congress</a> - then if you like those, I see no reason you should ever stop).</span>
  <a data-text-id="4320750920518755808" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_689067878" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/689067878" />
    <a title="Bob" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6991670-bob"><img alt="Bob" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545084294p2/6991670.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/689067878?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob" href="/user/show/6991670-bob">Bob</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6991670-bob?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer689067878" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14882438322755091216">Stanislaw Lem is a Polish science fiction writer who died in 2006. His writing is set in a futuristic, space travel setting but really explores the inner world of his characters. With Pirx, he explores in separate chapters that could more or less stand alone, phenomenon of lateral thinking and problem solving, sensory deprivation, the fatal mental errors we can make in stress situations, our urges to avoid anything associated with death and dying (Albatross), and the ways we (projected in this c</span>
  <span id="freeText14882438322755091216" style="display:none">Stanislaw Lem is a Polish science fiction writer who died in 2006. His writing is set in a futuristic, space travel setting but really explores the inner world of his characters. With Pirx, he explores in separate chapters that could more or less stand alone, phenomenon of lateral thinking and problem solving, sensory deprivation, the fatal mental errors we can make in stress situations, our urges to avoid anything associated with death and dying (Albatross), and the ways we (projected in this case upon a robot, Terminus) we carry around bits and snatches of significant dialogues. I don't know if there is a category of psychological fiction but Lem's work might fit here. One also feels at times that he writes with his tongue firmly planted in his cheek. There is a zany or quirky element to his stories that keep readers engaged.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14882438322755091216" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15751701667148571237">This was an interesting book. It wasn't an adventure-filled SF book with spaceships zooming hither and yon, but was rather more thoughty than I was expecting. Several of the stories ended rather abruptly, without a real resolution.<br/><br/>The writing itself was very interesting. It had a feel to it that wasn't like anything I'm used to. Was it really significantly different than I'm used to from SF writers from the US? Would I have noticed a difference if I didn't know Lem wasn't from the US? I don't kn</span>
  <span id="freeText15751701667148571237" style="display:none">This was an interesting book. It wasn't an adventure-filled SF book with spaceships zooming hither and yon, but was rather more thoughty than I was expecting. Several of the stories ended rather abruptly, without a real resolution.<br /><br />The writing itself was very interesting. It had a feel to it that wasn't like anything I'm used to. Was it really significantly different than I'm used to from SF writers from the US? Would I have noticed a difference if I didn't know Lem wasn't from the US? I don't know, but it definitely had an unusual feel to it.<br /><br />This book is definitely worth reading, but don't expect lots of aliens and lazzzers and the other trappings of space opera and skiffy.</span>

<div id="review_11106435" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11106435" />
    <a title="rob" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26372-rob"><img alt="rob" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1198794012p2/26372.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12286538069775790403">It's a bunch of short stories about this character, the eponymous Pirx. They're good science fiction and pretty good for short stories. Whatever, right?<br/><br/>They seem to repeat themes quite often, but they're sometimes funny and usually strange and the last story, terminus, is good even by short story standards. My short story standards are very high.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer640578694" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13956936235938869047">The future that wasn't and won't be, but interesting because imagined out of Poland. These are good stories, worth reading. <br/><br/>But when reading a book that has been translated from another language, do we enjoy the author's story or the interpretation of the author's words? </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_489637224" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/489637224" />
    <a title="John Defrog" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15533592-john-defrog"><img alt="John Defrog" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1359808978p2/15533592.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Defrog" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Defrog" href="/user/show/15533592-john-defrog">John Defrog</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer489637224" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17423467267179435840">A collection of stories that follow the career of Pirx, a space pilot top-loaded with self-doubt who somehow manages to get through one misadventure after another, often involving faulty technology and bureaucracy. The tales start from his days as a cadet, and end with the flight of a rickety old ship bound for Mars with a mysterious past and a robot that knows the fate of the previous crew. Lem can be a mixed bag, but I rather enjoyed this. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer639258428" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13823667995227559253">I got this in an ebook sale at Amazon a while back. I've been a fan of Lem for a long time, and I thought I'd read this back in high school, but when I read it this time none of it seemed familiar. The story isn't as humorous as something like <i>The Cyberiad</i> or as inventive as <i>The Futurological Congress</i>. The stories were still interesting, but newcomers would probably want to look to other books by Lem first.</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('J20RcDTyNN+CUMa+h898nydWd5K0N/sP9kczkIBx6XMJgae1Bm+VM7szCQCDHRZYGNLZCwBVlp4nEhJxMiXgpA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lMU67aq2AhkTiugAu182a7BmOk80nvfS/9Jylfotxp26KYwomCuj9SrpJ76/jVysj+KU1oD8mkMuh1N0SHnPSg==')})
  }

Originally published in Poland in 1937.<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7488539462230173070\" style=\"display:none\">In this bitterly funny novel by the renowned Polish author Witold Gombrowicz. a writer finds himself tossed into a chaotic world of schoolboys by a diabolical professor who wishes to reduce him to childishness. Originally published in Poland in 1937. Ferdydurke became an instant literary sensation and catapulted the young author to fame. Deemed scandalous and subversive by Nazis. Stalinists. and the Polish Communist regime in turn. the novel (as well as all of Gombrowicz\'s other works) was officially banned in Poland for decades. It has nonetheless remained one of the most influential works of twentieth-century European literature.  Ferdydurke is translated here directly from the Polish for the first time. Danuta Borchardt deftly captures Gombrowicz\'s playful and idiosyncratic style. and she allows English speakers to experience fully the masterpiece of a writer whom Milan Kundera describes as &quot;one of the great novelists of our century.&quot;  &quot;Extravagant. brilliant. disturbing. brave. funny-wonderful. . . . Long live its sublime mockery.&quot; ~ Susan Sontag. from the foreword  &quot;[A] masterpiece of European modernism. . . . Susan Sontag ushers this new translation into print with a strong and useful foreword. calling Gombrowicz\'s tale \'extravagant. brilliant. disturbing. brave. funny... wonderful.\' And it is.&quot; ~ Publishers Weekly  Witold Gombrowicz (1904-1969) wrote three other novels.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/10991.Stanis_aw_Lem">About Stanis≈Çaw Lem</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/10991.Stanis_aw_Lem"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1246185166p3/10991.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/10991.Stanis_aw_Lem">Stanis≈Çaw Lem
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7737022373288323147">Stanis≈Çaw Lem (staÀà…≤iswaf l…õm) was a Polish science fiction, philosophical and satirical writer of Jewish descent. His books have been translated into 41 languages and have sold over 27 million copies. He is perhaps best known as the author of <i>Solaris</i>, which has twice been made into a feature film. In 1976, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12531.Theodore_Sturgeon' title='Theodore Sturgeon' rel='nofollow noopener'>Theodore Sturgeon</a> claimed that Lem was the most widely read science-fiction writer in the w</span>
  <span id="freeText7737022373288323147" style="display:none">Stanis≈Çaw Lem (staÀà…≤iswaf l…õm) was a Polish science fiction, philosophical and satirical writer of Jewish descent. His books have been translated into 41 languages and have sold over 27 million copies. He is perhaps best known as the author of <i>Solaris</i>, which has twice been made into a feature film. In 1976, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12531.Theodore_Sturgeon" title="Theodore Sturgeon" rel="nofollow noopener">Theodore Sturgeon</a> claimed that Lem was the most widely read science-fiction writer in the world.<br /><br />His works explore philosophical themes; speculation on technology, the nature of intelligence, the impossibility of mutual communication and understanding, despair about human limitations and humankind's place in the universe. They are sometimes presented as fiction, but others are in the form of essays or philosophical books. Translations of his works are difficult and multiple translated versions of his works exist.<br /><br />Lem became truly productive after 1956, when the de-Stalinization period led to the "Polish October", when Poland experienced an increase in freedom of speech. Between 1956 and 1968, Lem authored 17 books. His works were widely translated abroad (although mostly in the Eastern Bloc countries). In 1957 he published his first non-fiction, philosophical book, <i>Dialogi</i> (Dialogues), one of his two most famous philosophical texts along with <i>Summa Technologiae</i> (1964). The Summa is notable for being a unique analysis of prospective social, cybernetic, and biological advances. In this work, Lem discusses philosophical implications of technologies that were completely in the realm of science fiction then, but are gaining importance today‚Äîlike, for instance, virtual reality and nanotechnology. Over the next few decades, he published many books, both science fiction and philosophical/futurological, although from the 1980s onwards he tended to concentrate on philosophical texts and essays.<br /><br />He gained international fame for <i>The Cyberiad</i>, a series of humorous short stories from a mechanical universe ruled by robots, first published in English in 1974. His best-known novels include <i>Solaris</i> (1961), <i>His Master's Voice</i> (G≈Ços pana, 1968), and the late <i>Fiasco</i> (Fiasko, 1987), expressing most strongly his major theme of the futility of mankind's attempts to comprehend the truly alien. Solaris was made into a film in 1972 by Russian director Andrei Tarkovsky and won a Special Jury Prize at the Cannes Film Festival in 1972; in 2002, Steven Soderbergh directed a Hollywood remake starring George Clooney.<br /><br />He was the cousin of poet <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5565396.Marian_Hemar" title="Marian Hemar" rel="nofollow noopener">Marian Hemar</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7737022373288323147" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
